Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
03 February 2024
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In today’s reality, where the global economy is constantly facing difficulties, the available
resources are depleting faster than ever before and the climatic change is becoming more
prevalent, drastic changes towards sustainability are necessary. In this thesis, different
methods of production for the feedstocks needed by the Uloop (fig. 1) for the synthesis
of 1 [tSCP /h] will be studied and compared with the current situation. More specifically,
three scenarios were studied with the main difference among them being the production of
methane and the similarity being the utilization of an innovative electrochemical ammonia
synthesis pathway from Nitrovolt.
Figure 1: Feedstocks for Uloop reactor
The scenarios researched, are anaerobic digestion and amine carbon capture for the ac­
quisition of a bioMethane stream with the carbon dioxide being sold as revenue (case S1),
catalytic methanation of a syngas stream towards eMethane (case S2), and lastly a com­
bination between anaerobic digestion and biogas upgrading via biological methanation
in an archaea reactor (case S3). For the comparison of these different cases, process
design, life cycle, and feasibility models were created for a complete techno­economic
analysis to be achieved.
Out of the three options, the most promising near­term solution was S1 mostly because
it showcased the smallest price increase of 186 $/tSCP ] more than the base case price
of 2105.62 [$/tSCP ]. In addition to that, S1 compensated for this +9 [%] increase in price
with an important reduction of the carbon intensity (CI) score of the product, reducing the
current calculated standard of 7.71 [tCO2/tSCP ] by 23 [%] to 5.96 [tCO2/tSCP ]. Lastly, case
one had the greatest energy efficiency out of the three options at 16 [%].
In contrast to case one, S2 is by far the worst option for the small­scale operation mod­
eled. Many reasons contributed to that with the key ones being the polymer membrane
electrolysis (PEM) unit is needed with a capacity of almost 50 [MW] along with the catalytic
methanation facility. The PEM unit increased the dependence on electricity significantly,
while also both systems suffer from high operational and capital costs which resulted in a
minimum selling price of 4174.73 [$/tSCP ] for the final product.
Finally, S3 similarly to S2 is very dependent on the electricity price and availability, despite
that it has great potential for long­term utilization mostly due to the much lower carbon
intensity (CI) of 2.58 [tCO2,eq/tSCP ] it achieved. As a result of that, under the right circum­
stances of low electricity (20­25 [$/MWh]) and the materialization of green incentives (100
$/tCO2,eq) S3 results in a minimum selling price of 2094.41 [$/tSCP ] lower than the baseline
value established before.
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
iii
==PAGE:6==
Evaluating the results yielded, sustainable production pathways are slowly but steadily
closing the gap between them and the fossil­originating chemicals, mostly because of
the potential incentivisation of their significant environmental benefits. For this gap to be
bridged sooner, more work needs to be done in the areas of renewable electricity produc­
tion and provision and green project financing. Regarding the current the key next steps
are the implementation of a heat integration plan that will drive up the energy efficiency
of the system and also the conduction of a more robust life cycle assessment to validate
or update the generated results.
iv
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production

1
Introduction
With the interest in renewable energy and sustainability increasing, terms like Power to X,
bioenergy, and industrial symbiosis become more and more intriguing and interconnected.
For this reason, this Master’s Thesis aims to investigate the sustainable production path­
ways for chemicals with many uses, one of which being their consumption toward protein
synthesis.
With that in mind, the problem at hand is the production of ammonia, methane, and oxygen
from air, water, electricity, and biomass instead of fossil fuels. From there these three
chemicals will be fed in the Uloop fermentor, designed by Unibio, out of which high­quality
Uniprotein can be produced.
The belief is that this substitution will result in a significant reduction of greenhouse gas
emissions not only due to the minimizing of fossil during the production process but also
the transportation of the feedstocks. Unfortunately, as with every green project, this re­
duction is probably going to increase the cost of production of Uniprotein, but at the same
time, it will offer the opportunity to investigate how much the environmental impact can be
valued for the different scenarios to have the same financial viability in the end.
Out of the three chemicals of interest, methane production from sustainable sources has
been well documented in the past few years with anaerobic digestion and methanation
processes being implemented already in medium and large scale. Oxygen is abundant
in nature so its sourcing is easy to guarantee. Ammonia, on the other hand, has been
produced in the same unsustainable way for the past 100 years and currently, this market
generates around 2 [%] of global emissions and consumes about 2 [%] of the total en­
ergy generated [1]. Here came Nitrovolt with their revolutionary electrochemical ammonia
production pathway, which their currently researching, and provided the spark behind the
following project.
Finally, the inspiration behind this Master’s Thesis was the world’s leading industrial sym­
biosis plant in Kalundborg, Denmark [2] with the main principle behind industrial symbiosis
being that the residual flows of one process can become a resource in another, resulting
in the sharing and reusing of resources along with environmental and financial benefits
[3].

2
Literature Review
Two main technologies constitute the foundation of this work are, the ULoop® fermen­
tor developed by Unibio for protein synthesis [4] and the decentralized electrochemical
ammonia production facility currently being researched by Nitrovolt® [5].
Essentially,
ammonia (NH3) produced from water, air, and renewable electricity via the Nitrovolot
process will be fed along with sustainably produced methane (CH4), oxygen (O2), wa­
ter (H2O) and minerals required for the flourishing of the bacteria (available in table A.2
at appendix A.1.1) in the ULoop®. The result of this process is a concentrated protein
product, that can be used as feed for animals, known as Uniprotein®.
This product is both free from toxins, thanks to the controlled production process and
the use of food­grade minerals, and is not genetically manipulated, being the outcome
of a natural process. Moreover, Uniprotein® has undergone testing as feed for various
animals, with positive results in terms of acceptance and growth. In summary, Uniprotein®
serves as a viable substitute for high­quality fish meal and can also replace soybean meal,
both of which are becoming increasingly scarce resources. The subsequent sections
will delve into the Uloop process along with the different technologies selected for the
production of the feedstocks needed.
2.1
Protein Synthesis
Unibio is a pioneering force in the bioenergy sector, thanks to its distinctive fermentation
technology developed in collaboration with DTU (Technical University of Denmark) [4].
The process involves a fermentor and methane­consuming bacteria that undergo multi­
plication under optimal conditions. A visual representation of the different steps followed
for the Uniprotein synthesis can be seen in fig. 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Uniprotein® production process
The Uloop’s outlet stream consists of the protein broth, water, carbon dioxide (CO2), and
the unreacted portions of methane (CH4) and oxygen (O2). The liquid mixture of water
and protein broth undergoes processing initially in a separator, which extracts water for
reuse, aiming to minimize the subsequent drying requirements in the process. On the
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
3
==PAGE:20==
other hand, the gaseous mixture of the generated carbon dioxide, along with any remain­
ing oxygen and methane is mixed with a surplus of air and combusted to generate the
energy needed for the processing of the liquid stream. The flue gas produced by the
combustion must be treated before it is discharged into the atmosphere for the total emis­
sions of the system to be reduced, but currently, this is not happening.
Following fig. 2.1, after the separator, an evaporator is used, where low pressures enable
water to evaporate at significantly lower operating temperatures. After this stage, the
liquid stream undergoes homogenization and heat treatment before entering the spray
drying chamber. At this juncture, Uniprotein emerges as the final product. Among these
units, only the U­loop will be integrated into the Aspen models, while the others will solely
contribute to the financial analysis.
2.1.1
U­loop Fermentor
In conventional biological processes that involve mixing two or more biological substances,
a stirred reactor tank is typically employed. However, for the task of producing the single­
cell Uniprotein (SCP), the stirred reactor tank is substituted by the U­Loop fermentor,
which shares similarities with a fermentation tank and was designed to offer optimal grow­
ing conditions for bacteria. When it is assumed that methane, oxygen, and ammonia are
fully converted towards SCP the chemical reaction occurring takes the form of [6].
CH4 + 1, 45O2 + 0, 104NH3 →0, 52CH1,8O0,5 N0,2 + 0, 48CO2 + 1, 69H2O
(2.1)
with: CH1.8O0.5N0.2 being the assumed simplified chemical composition of the protein.
However, this is not a realistic case. For this reason, a utilization rate of 90% for oxygen
and 80% for methane is assumed for the production of protein and other by­products
based on work performed by Rasmus Abildgaard Fischer [7]. With the utilization rates
set, eq. (2.2) presents a more realistic reaction, where methane and oxygen can also
be found on the product side of the reaction to showcase the amount that has not been
converted.
1, 25CH4 + 1, 61O2 + 0, 104NH3 →
0, 52CH1,8O0,5 N0,2 + 0, 48CO2 + 1, 69H2O + 0, 25CH4 + 0, 16O2
(2.2)
Despite the reactions suggesting that the protein product comprises solely of carbon,
hydrogen, oxygen, and nitrogen, this representation is not entirely accurate. The reaction
formulas outlined in this report have been simplified, as the amounts of other elements,
available in table A.1 at appendix A.1.1, are present in such minute quantities that they
can be disregarded. Other pivotal assumptions regarding the Uloop process are available
below:
• Ammonia utilization is 100%
• There is a steady flow through the U­loop and it is not batch­operated
• The substances that are needed in low quantities will not be included in the modeling
aspect but will contribute to the operational costs.
• Each U­Loop is assumed to have a protein production rate of 200 kg/h and a power
consumption of 160 kW.

2.1.2
Feedstock
With the Uloop described and the remaining units being based on previous work [7], [8],
the next field of interest is the understanding of the production processes for the three
chemicals. First of all ammonia, a compound composed of hydrogen and nitrogen that
plays a pivotal role in various industrial processes. Nowadays ammonia is mainly syn­
thesized through the Haber Bosch process, where hydrogen derived from natural gas
and nitrogen from the air are combined at very high operating conditions. However, ad­
vancements in technology and environmental concerns have led to the exploration of new
methods for ammonia production, focusing mainly on sustainable hydrogen generation
but also a potential electrochemical synthesis pathway.
Another key component is methane, vital in many other industrial applications and gener­
ated through a variety of pathways. Traditionally, methane is obtained from natural gas,
but sustainable alternatives are gaining traction. The most technology­ready options that
can produce significant amounts of methane are anaerobic digestion of organic waste,
catalytic methanation of CO2 and H2, and its biological alternative. All these substitutes
of natural gas help with the mitigation of greenhouse gasses and the fostering of a circular
carbon economy.
Lastly, oxygen is an essential element for various industrial and life­supporting processes.
It is primarily sourced from oxygen­generating facilities that utilize advanced separation
technologies to extract it from the air. Another option that is gaining traction is water
electrolysis. The adoption of sustainable methods of production aligns with the global
shift towards cleaner and more eco­friendly industrial practices. The following chapters
will focus on the different pathways, researched and selected for the production of these
substances.
2.2
Hydrogen production
Hydrogen, the simplest element in the universe, holds immense potential as a clean
and sustainable energy source. Its abundance, energy density, and versatility make it
a promising candidate for addressing the world’s energy needs while minimizing envi­
ronmental impact. When combusted, hydrogen produces only water vapor, eliminating
harmful greenhouse gas emissions associated with fossil fuels [9]. This clean combus­
tion characteristic makes hydrogen a compelling option for powering vehicles, generating
electricity, and heating homes and businesses. Moreover, hydrogen can act as an energy
storage medium, efficiently storing renewable energy generated during periods of surplus
to be utilized during peak demand [10]. As the world transitions towards a low­carbon
future, green hydrogen is poised to play a pivotal role in decarbonizing various sectors,
reducing reliance on fossil fuels, and mitigating climate change.
Currently, steam reforming of methane and other hydrocarbons is the predominant method
for hydrogen generation. While this process boasts a low production cost it comes with
unacceptably high carbon dioxide emissions, typically between 8 and 10 kg of carbon
dioxide (CO2) per kg of hydrogen H2 [10]. As a result, there is a growing global inter­
est in alternative options with lower emissions. Addressing this challenge requires the
formulation of plans encompassing near­, mid­, and long­term solutions, ensuring both a
lowered environmental footprint and feasibility for the investors. The financial goals that
are currently being set regarding green projects usually assume governmental monetary
aid, for the sustainable technologies to become cost­competitive with their conventional
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
5
==PAGE:22==
fossil counterparts [11],[12].
2.2.1
Promissing technologies
In the near­/mid­term, water electrolysis is poised to meet financial targets, under the
belief that electricity prices will reduce and further technological advancements will be
achieved [13]. In addition, gasification of biomass and anaerobic digestion present two
more potential options of well­established technologies that can be leveraged to yield
hydrogen along with carbon­containing streams from residues, waste, and biomass. An­
other possible pathway is the replacement of natural gas in the steam reforming process
by hydrocarbons that are generated from sustainable processes, like first and second­
generation ethanol [14]. Of course, the ideas regarding biomass utilization for hydrogen
production are less attractive because of the biogenic carbon atoms and the higher costs
of hydrogen production [15].
Regarding the long­term solutions, advancements in waste stream treatment through
dark­/photo­fermentation are expected to result in significant hydrogen yields while si­
multaneously dealing with the constantly increasing amount of squandering [16]. Another
suggestion is the utilization of solar energy for photoelectrochemical processes or bio­
photolysis [17]. In these scenarios, either via a semiconductor material that absorbs sun­
light, to generate electrons or through photosynthetic organisms, such as microalgae and
cyanobacteria sunlight can be harnessed towards hydrogen generation. Finally, thermo­
chemical water splitting, where the excess heat from nuclear power plants could be uti­
lized, is being researched [18]. Despite these long­term ideas being very innovative and
having plenty of environmental benefits, their low technology readiness level (TRL) and
hydrogen yields leave them noncompetitive at the moment. This wide variety of suggested
pathways, gathered in fig. 2.2, reflects the ongoing commitment towards sustainability.
Figure 2.2: Sustainable pathways for hydrogen production [19], [20]
During a special course, a comprehensive literature review of these options was con­
ducted resulting in the distinction of the most promising technologies based on their TRL,
hydrogen yield, generated emissions, and estimated levelized cost of production (LCoP)
[21]. Out of all the aforementioned options water electrolysis was selected as the best
current alternative to SMR [22]. During electrolysis, an external circuit powered by re­
newable electricity, provides the driving force, in the form of cell voltage, for the cell to
6
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:23==
split water (H2O) into high­purity hydrogen (H2) and oxygen (O2) [23]. The reaction that
is being described is:
H2O + Energy →2H+ + 2e−+ 1/2O2 →H2 + 1/2O2
(2.3)
Ongoing research is dedicated to identifying efficient combinations of renewable energy
sources, such as wind turbines, solar panels, and bioenergy, to achieve cost­effective
electricity production [10], [24]. This research aligns with projections that foresee a de­
crease in electricity prices, further solidifying water electrolysis as a viable and sustainable
avenue for hydrogen production in the coming years. At its forefront, there are three dis­
tinct technologies: alkaline electrolysis (AE), polymer electrolyte membrane electrolysis
(PEM), and solid oxide electrolysis (SOEC).
2.2.2
Alkaline Electrolysis ­ AE
Overall, alkaline water electrolysis is the most well­established commercially available
water technology. It operates at relatively lower temperatures around 80­100 [oC] but
suffers from limited current densities, low operating pressure, and low energy efficiency
[25]. It utilizes two electrodes (anode and cathode) commonly made of nickel that are
soaked in an alkaline solution of potassium hydroxide (KOH) or sodium hydroxide (NaOH)
at a concentration of 20­30 [%]. The electrodes are separated by a porous diaphragm
(made from asbestos) or membrane [26]. The anode is the positively charged electrode
where anions, in this case, hydroxides (OH−), are attracted and oxidation occurs:
2OH−→H2O + 1/2O2 + 2e−
(2.4)
On the other hand, the cathode is negatively charged, attracts the electrons, and is also
the electrode where reduction takes place:
2H2O + 2e−→H2 + 2OH−
(2.5)
An illustration of the flows in an alkaline electrolysis cell is available in fig. 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Schematic illustration of alkaline electrolysis [27]
2.2.3
Polymer membrane electrolysis ­ PEM
The polymer membrane electrolysis was developed to overcome the drawbacks of al­
kaline water electrolysis. Some of the main advantages of this technology are its low
environmental impact, high purity of the hydrogen stream, lower operating temperatures
(50­80 [oC]), high current density, and therefore high energy efficiency. In addition, the
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
7
==PAGE:24==
fast response to changes from the system and the ease of balancing it make it very com­
patible with renewable electricity sources [28]. On the other hand, the technology comes
with an increased production cost, lower lifetime, and great sensitivity towards impurities.
With that being said, in PEM electrolysis, a solid polymer electrolyte (SPE), alternatively
known as proton exchange membrane (Nafion), is used for the facilitation of protons (H+)
between the anode and cathode, and the separation of the produced hydrogen and oxy­
gen gases. The benefits of the Nafion membrane are its lower gas permeability, high
proton conductivity, lower thickness, and high­pressure operations. The anode in this
electrolysis type is typically made of an iridium oxide (IrO2) catalyst which is highly ac­
tive for the oxygen evolution reaction (OER), while simultaneously being relatively stable
and resistant to corrosion. Regarding the cathode, a platinum­based catalyst is usually
selected since it’s highly active for the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) and does not
corrode by water or other electrolytes [29]. However, platinum is expensive, so alternative
catalysts, such as nickel, are sometimes used to drive down the cost. The occurring reac­
tions in the anode and cathode along with a visual representation of the system (fig. 2.5)
are available below.
Anode:H2O →2H+ + 1/2O2 + 2e−
(2.6)
Cathode:2H+ + 2e−→H2
(2.7)
Figure 2.4: Schematic illustration of polymer membrane electrolysis [27]
2.2.4
Solid oxide electrolysis ­ SOEC
In the last few years, the technology has rapidly gained traction, particularly in projects
geared toward green ammonia and methanol production thanks to the high purity of the
produced hydrogen stream, energy efficiency as well as the great amount of heat that
can be integrated into such projects [30]. The main characteristic of SOEC is the higher
operating temperature which is advantageous for hydrogen production but makes the
technology struggle with stability and degradation.
SOEC usually operates between 600–1000 [oC] and utilizes water in the form of steam. A
solid oxide electrolyte (SOE) is used to facilitate the transportation of oxygen ions (O2−)
between the anode and cathode, which are made of a lanthanum strontium manganite
(LSM) and a Ni­YSZ cermet correspondingly [31]. LSM is a highly conductive material for
oxygen ions, while also being stable at high operating temperatures. Similarly, Ni­YSZ
is a highly conductive material for electrons, while also being compatible with the LSM
8
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:25==
anode and able to withstand high temperatures. The reactions and operating principle of
this electrolysis are:
Anode:O2−→1/2O2 + 2e−
(2.8)
Cathode:H2O + 2e−→H2 + O2−
(2.9)
Figure 2.5: Schematic illustration of solid oxide electrolysis [27]
2.2.5
Comparison
Summarizing the above, each technology boasts unique characteristics and applications,
offering a diverse range of solutions for hydrogen production. Alkaline electrolysis stands
out for its maturity and cost­effectiveness but struggles with efficiency. Polymer mem­
brane electrolysis is more energy efficient (up to 70 [%]) and easily adaptable to fluctuat­
ing electricity demands but has a higher cost of production and is sensitive to impurities.
Finally, solid oxide electrolyzers stand out as a promising future pathway due to their ex­
ceptional efficiency, ability to utilize both water and air simultaneously, and potential for
heat integration in various industrial applications. Some of the most important character­
istics of each technology are gathered in table 2.1. Concluding this section, for the current
project, the high flexibility of PEM makes it the ideal candidate for the technologies and
combinations that are going to be used.

2.3
Nitrogen production
Despite the abundance of nitrogen (N2) in the air, constituting about 78% by volume, its
practical availability is hindered by its mixture with other gases, which often have unde­
sirable effects on industrial processes [33], [34]. Nevertheless, the presence of nitrogen
in the air offers a readily available source, eliminating the need for intricate chemical syn­
thesis. Several methods are currently being employed to extract nitrogen from air for
industrial purposes with the standard methods being Cryogenic Distillation or Air Separa­
tion (ASU), Pressure Swing Adsorption (PSA), and Membrane permeation [35], [36],[37].
Each method offers distinct advantages depending on the scale, desired purity, and in­
dustry of implementation.
2.3.1
Cryogenic distillation ­ ASU
Cryogenic distillation also known as Air Separation (ASU), is currently the main nitrogen­
producing technology in large­scale industries [38]. Two distillation columns operate at
very low temperatures to liquefy air and selectively separate its key components (nitrogen,
oxygen, and argon) according to their different boiling points. In this process, the inlet air
must first be pretreated for the removal of dust particles and moisture that could freeze in
the process [39], [40].
The pretreated stream of clean compressed air is further cooled via a counter­flow heat
exchange with outlet streams and by passing through an expansion turbine. At this point,
the liquefied air goes through the distillation columns, at a temperature slightly below the
boiling point (BP) of the oxygen taking advantage of the difference in the nitrogen and oxy­
gen BP (see fig. A.1 at appendix A.1.3). The first, High­Pressure Column (HPC) operates
at the same pressure as the inlet stream (6 [bar]), while the second one (LPC) at around
1.5 [bar] [41]. The generated streams from the first distillation rich in nitrogen (distillate)
and oxygen (bottom) go through expanders to reach the lower desired pressure and then
are fed on different stages in the second column. From there, high­purity (99.99%) nitro­
gen product emerges as distillate and high­purity O2 is produced in the bottom [42].
Figure 2.6: Cryogenic air distillation process [41]
Capacities of on­site cryogenic nitrogen plants range from 250 to over 50,000 [Nm3/h]
depending on the demand of the industry [41]. Cryogenic distillation benefits a lot from
economies of scale when compared with the two alternative options that will follow and
10
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:27==
for this reason, ASU constitutes the industrial standard for large­scale air separation and
can potentially reach 150,000 [tonsN2/year].
2.3.2
Mechanical Methods
In contrast with cryogenic distillation, mechanical methods such as Membrane Perme­
ation and Pressure Swing Adsorption (PSA), exploit the distinct physical properties of the
component gases of air to facilitate nitrogen extraction. The main benefits of these path­
ways are the reduced complexity, compact design, and potential to be financially viable
for on­site small and medium­scale nitrogen production thanks to their low operating costs
[43].
Pressure Swing Adsorption ­ PSA
PSA is a continuous cyclic separation process that consists of separating nitrogen from
air by adsorbing the oxygen on a solid under pressure. The process consists of two main
stages, which are operated concurrently and rely on the preferential adsorption and des­
orption of air components by a molecular carbon sieve. In the adsorption step, oxygen,
vapor, and other impurities are removed from a pressurized air stream, allowing nitrogen
to flow toward a storage unit [41]. This continues until the sieve is fully saturated. In the
desorption step, the process is reversed, releasing the trapped oxygen. This regenerates
the molecular sieve, preparing it for a new cycle. The sieves of the two stages operate at
near­ambient temperatures and pressures of 6­8 [bar] for adsorption and 1 [bar] for the
oxygen release phase [44].
Figure 2.7: Pressure swing adsorption process [41]
These milder operating conditions along with the fact that PSA units are compact, very
flexible, and inherently modular, render the technology attractive, especially for small pro­
duction scales below 7,500 [tonN2/day]. The main drawbacks of PSA, when compared to
ASU, include being more expensive when scaled up, achieving lower nitrogen purity at
99.8% [44] (need for an extra purification unit), and having higher energy consumption.
However, the long­lasting operation of the system with proper maintenance makes it a
durable and on­site alternative.
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
11
==PAGE:28==
Membrane permeation
The separation is driven by the selective permeation of molecules across the membrane
wall and the pressure difference between the feed which is at 6­25 [bar] and the per­
meate which is at atmospheric pressure. This process is carried out at a near ambient
temperature and polysulfone hollow fiber or carbon molecular sieve membranes are nor­
mally used [41]. The inner and outer surfaces of the membranes are separated by a thin
porous layer which allows nitrogen molecules to pass through due to their smaller size
and lower interaction with the membrane material [45].
Figure 2.8: Membrane permeation process [41]
This method offers several advantages over the other two options, including a modular
configuration and low capital cost, better dynamic flexibility than ASU, and simpler oper­
ation than PSA, while also producing less greenhouse gas emissions during production.
Its drawback is that for nitrogen purity above 99.5% to be achieved, very high pressure is
required which results in high energy consumption. Additionally, membrane permeation
struggles financially with scalability and for this reason small scale nitrogen production of
around 750 [tonN2/day] [41], [46].
2.3.3
Comparison
Summarizing the previous sections, the selection of a nitrogen­producing technology de­
pends on the required nitrogen purity, scale of application, flexibility, and cost. With that
said, table 2.2 has the key information gathered for each pathway. Concluding, given the
small­scale facility that will be researched in this project, along with the desired system
from Nitrovolt, membrane permeation is selected.

2.4
Ammonia Synthesis
Ammonia, commonly used as a refrigerant, fertilizer, and chemical feedstock, is now being
considered a sustainable energy carrier. As the global population and uses of ammonia
increase, so does the demand for greater production. Even though ammonia has been
produced on an industrial scale for more than 100 years [47], the complexity of the pro­
duction process, due to its high energy demand, remains. This high energy requirement
is mainly due to how hard the breakdown of the diatomic nitrogen triple bond is. Currently,
over 96 [%] of global ammonia production relies on the Haber Bosch (HB) synthesis pro­
cess [42].
2.4.1
Haber Bosch
In this large­scale centralized process, hydrogen and nitrogen are combined using an
iron oxide catalyst under high pressure and temperature. While the initial HB process
plant had a production capacity of 20 [tons/day], ammonia has since become the second
most­produced chemical globally, reaching approximately 180 [mil. tons/year] in 2021
[41]. Over the years, optimization efforts, including the use of different catalysts and
engineering advancements, have reduced the energy consumption of the Haber Bosch
process from 110 [kWh/kg] to 7.7–10.1 [kWh/kg]. Despite these improvements, ammonia
production still consumes about 2 [%] of the total primary energy [48], [49]. Addition­
ally, a notable drawback of this extensively used process is its environmental impact.
Nowadays HB generates over 420 million tons of carbon dioxide annually contributing to
about 2 [%] of the world’s CO2 emissions. This translates to approximately 2.5 tonnes
of CO2 per tonne of ammonia through the conventional method where hydrogen, from
steam methane reforming (SMR), accounts for almost 90 [%] of the total emissions [50],
[1]. Another significant issue is the potential methane leakage in different stages of the
production that comes with very unfortunate environmental impacts.
The overall mechanism of ammonia generation includes the absorption and dissociation
of N2 and H2 from the catalyst, the production of NH3 on the catalyst’s surface, and its
dissolution. It is noteworthy that the rate­determining step in the entire process is the
dissociation of the nitrogen molecule [51]. The reaction steps are as follows:
rate determining step: N2 + 2∗→2 N∗
(2.10)
H2 + 2∗−→2H∗
(2.11)
N∗+ H∗→NH∗+ ∗
(2.12)
NH∗+ H∗→NH∗
2 + ∗
(2.13)
NH2 ∗+H∗→NH∗
3 + ∗
(2.14)
NH∗
3 →NH3 + ∗
(2.15)
Overall reaction:N2 + 3H2 →2NH3
(2.16)
In a typical HB system where the not­reacting stream of H2 and N2 is recycled and mixed
with the makeup stream, which fixes the ratios between the molecules, before entering
the reactor again. This can be seen in fig. 2.9. Because of this constant recycling, high
ammonia yields can be achieved. [52]
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
13
==PAGE:30==
Figure 2.9: Haber Bosch schematic representation [41]
Despite HB’s cost­effectiveness in large­scale applications, the environmental impact as­
sociated with the process along with the high operating conditions (usually between 400­
500 [oC] and 150­350 [bar]) underlines the need for a drastic shift towards more sustain­
able ammonia production processes. Most suggestions have to do with the sustainable
acquisition of hydrogen, while some others have a more innovative approach and enter­
tain the idea of new synthesis pathways.
Although sustainable hydrogen generation is imperative for the future, pathways achiev­
ing that already exist as was established in section 2.2. Therefore, alternative ammonia
production possibilities are not only very interesting but also of high significance. Cur­
rently, there is no commercially available ammonia synthesis system other than HB, but
some of the proposed options include solar thermochemical looping and photochemical
or electrochemical ammonia synthesis [47]. Out of these options, the last one was the
basis behind Nitrovolt but also the spark behind this thesis [53].
2.4.2
Electrochemical ammonia synthesis
Electrochemical N2 reduction reaction (NRR) offers a renewable and distributed route for
NH3 production. A great benefit of the process is that it can operate at ambient tem­
perature and a pressure range between 10 to 20 [bar]. The process takes place in an
electrode­electrolyte interface and requires protons transferred from the electrolyte, elec­
trons transferred from the electrode (catalyst), and optimized sites on the electrode sur­
face onto which N2 molecules can be adsorbed and subsequently activated [53].
Among electrochemical routes, metal­mediated nitrogen reduction reaction (Li­NRR) is
currently the only method proven to produce ammonia. The mechanism for the Li­NRR
process involves a plethora of steps including the electroplating of metallic Li on cathode
substrates in the Li+ containing organic electrolyte. After that the activation of nitrogen
on the surface of metallic Li follows, resulting in the formation of surface lithium nitride
(Li3N). In the absence of a proton source (it can be renewable Hydrogen, EtOH, etc.
[54]), this lithium metal becomess unreactive. Finally, the protonation of surface lithium
nitride by proton sources (H+) occurs resulting in the generation of ammonia and Li+. A
visual representation of these steps is available in fig. 2.10. As can be seen there, the
cyclic nature of the process means that ideally Li+ is reused and not consumed during
the process, which results in its high overall sustainability and tolerable efficiency [55].
14

==PAGE:31==

Nitrovolt
Based on this principle Nitrovolt created an ammonia­producing configuration available at
a lab scale at the moment. The three units that will constitute the system are PEM elec­

nitrolyzer that electrochemically generates ammonia. The goal of Nitrovolt is the creation
of container­sized units capable of producing 150 [kgNH3/d] resulting in a decentralized
production pathway that makes ammonia available to everyone. The following fig. 2.11

• Significantly lower carbon intensity score,

• Modular, decentralized, and small­scale operation (ideally near renewable electricity
sources),
• Scalability and on­demand NH3 production,
• Flexible operation thanks to the low temperature that allows quick ramp up and
down.
2.5
Oxygen production
Oxygen is a highly reactive nonmetal, due to its two unpaired electrons in its outermost
electron shell, which forms oxides with most elements. In addition to that, oxygen is
essential for life, as it plays a crucial role in many biological processes, including respira­
tion, and photosynthesis. It is also used in a variety of industrial processes, such as steel
making, fertilizer production, and water purification [57]. Oxygen generation is the pro­
cess of separating oxygen from water, air, or other gas mixtures, and the main pathways
for achieving that include water electrolysis, cryogenic distillation, pressure swing adsorp­
tion, membrane utilization, and chemical oxygen generators (which was not investigated).
These technologies have already been described in section 2.2 and section 2.3.
2.6
Methane Synthesis
Methane is a simple hydrocarbon composed of one carbon atom and four hydrogen
atoms. It plays a crucial role in various industrial, commercial, and domestic applications.
As the primary component of natural gas, methane serves as a vital energy source for
heating, electricity generation, and industrial processes. However, concerns about natu­
ral gas’ environmental impact, especially the fugitive methane emissions during extraction
and transportation, have led to a growing interest in sustainable methane production.
2.6.1
Anaerobic Digestion
In the absence of oxygen, microorganisms break down organic materials into biogas, a
renewable energy source that holds immense potential for environmental sustainability
and waste management. This naturally occurring process for biogas production finds ap­
plications in various sectors, including agriculture, wastewater treatment, and industrial
waste management. Anaerobic digestion has four distinct stages which are Hydrolysis,
Acidogenesis, Acetogenesis, and Methanogenesis, a series of biochemical reactions or­
chestrated by microorganisms.
During hydrolysis complex insoluble organic molecules like carbohydrates, proteins, and
fats are broken down into simple soluble molecules like sugars, amino acids, and fatty
acids. These simpler molecules then enter the acidogenesis phase where they are con­
verted into carbon dioxide, hydrogen, and short­chain organic acids, such as acetate,
propionate, and butyrate. The third step, called acetogenesis, is the conversion of the
short­chain organic acids into acetic acid, hydrogen, and carbon dioxide. Finally, during
Methanogenesis, certain single­celled organisms called methanogens convert the inter­
mediate products, namely acetic acid, hydrogen, and carbon dioxide into biogas (primarily
methane and carbon dioxide). This methane­rich gas can be either directly combusted to
generate electricity or heat, or upgraded to pipeline­quality biomethane [58], [59].
The anaerobic digestion process usually operates at mild conditions with the optimum
temperature range being between 35­45 [oC] for mesophilic operation and 50­60 [oC] for
16
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:33==
thermophilic which leads to faster biogas production but also requires more energy input
for the elevated temperature to be maintained. Another significant characteristic of AD is
the time required for microorganisms to break down the organic matter or hydraulic re­
tention time (HRT). HRT is influenced by the solid content of the feedstock which can be
categorized as total (TS) and volatile (VS) solids. TS corresponds to the total amount of
matter, both organic and inorganic, present in a sample while VS is an organic subset of
TS [58].
Finally, anaerobic digestion also yields valuable byproducts composing the digestate as
in Figure 3.3. Liquid digestate, a nutrient­rich mixture of organic compounds, can be used
as fertilizer or soil conditioner, enhancing soil fertility and crop productivity. Solid diges­
tate, while less nutrient­rich, can be utilized as animal feed or compost, reducing the need
for chemical fertilizers and promoting soil health. Concluding this chapter, the benefits of
anaerobic digestion extend beyond energy production and waste management, it’s a pro­
cess that can contribute to a circular economy, reduce waste while producing value, and
help with the transition to renewable energy sources.
2.6.2
Biological Methanation
Similarly to the last step of anaerobic digestion, in the biological methanation process,
methanogenic bacteria catalyze the conversion of carbon dioxide and hydrogen towards
methane, in the absence of oxygen. Streams fed to this biological reactor can be biogas
mixed with pure hydrogen or syngas stream of pure hydrogen and captured CO2. Usu­
ally, a continuous stirred tank reactor (CSTR), called Archaea, is used for better mixing
between the bacteria and the feedstock to be achieved. In addition, the cooling needs
of this exothermic process can be covered by cooling water streams due to the low op­
erating temperature (50­60 [oC]) [60]. This medium­scale methanation is an efficient and
also economical pathway thanks to the reactor’s stability, flexible operation, and low in­
vestment and operational costs. The following equation showcases the methanation of
CO2 and H2,
CO2 + 4H2 =⇒CH4 + 2H2O + heat
(2.20)
Figure 2.12: Drawing of archaea reactor [7]
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
17
==PAGE:34==
2.6.3
Catalytical Methanation
Finally, the catalytic methanation of H2 and CO2 towards CH4 is researched. The effi­
ciency of the process hinges on several critical factors, including the following, catalyst
composition, operating temperature, and pressure. Catalyst selection is crucial for the
right activity and selectivity to be achieved with the most commonly used being nickel (Ni)
or ruthenium (Ru). Moreover, although elevated temperatures enhance reaction rates
(potential range between 200 and 800 [oC]), excessive temperatures can lead to catalyst
deactivation and changes in product selectivity with the reverse water gas shift (rWGS)
reaction becoming more prevalent. Finally, high pressure also influences the methana­
tion process (acceptable values between 1 and 100 [bar]), affecting the balance between
reactant and product concentrations. Based on the work by Manuel Bailera et al [61]
regarding methane yield at the thermodynamic equilibrium for varying temperatures and
pressures, the following fig. 2.13 was produced.
Figure 2.13: Theoretical CO2 conversion, CH4 selectivity, and CH4 yield [61].

3
Methods
At this point, the presentation of the idea begins. The ultimate goal is the utilization of
sustainably produced ammonia, methane, and oxygen for the synthesis of Uniprotein®.
For this reason, different, scenarios are designed. More precisely, each of the following
cases will investigate possible combinations of available technologies that can yield the
desired inlet products in a feasible and environmentally friendly way.
3.1
Idea formulation
The baseline of the thesis is the conventional approach which is labeled (case S0). Here
ammonia is produced via the Haber Bosch process, methane comes as natural gas, and
oxygen is generated from Air Separation. This case is not going to be modeled in Aspen
Plus since the only process included is the conversion of these feedstocks to SCP, but
SimaPro and Feasibility models will be made. A visual representation of S0 can be seen
in fig. 3.1. This case is expected to have the lowest cost of production, but a significantly
higher environmental impact.
Figure 3.1: Unibio process scheme [6]
Regarding the suggested alternatives, the patent from Nitrovolt will be implemented in all
the following cases for the production of ammonia. With that being said, the first sug­
gestion (case S1) is the production of biomethane from the combination of an AD facility
with an amine carbon capture unit for purification. The generated side stream of CO2, is
going to be an extra revenue stream (BECCS) and not be utilized for the production of
any carbon­containing chemical in the facility. Lastly, the oxygen demand is covered by
a combination of an oxygen­generating unit (ASU) and the side stream of oxygen coming
out of the water electrolysis unit included in the Nitrobox®.
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
19
==PAGE:36==
Following S1, the next case (S2) focuses on the production of ”eProteins”. More specifi­
cally, the CO2 contained in the exhaust fumes (flue gas) from the Uloop will be extracted
by an amine carbon capture unit. This CO2 stream along with carbon dioxide provided by
other biological processes, outside of the scope of this project (BECCS) will be fed along
with hydrogen from water electrolysis to a catalytic methanation unit. Given the synthetic
nature of both ammonia and methane and the fact that the oxygen is generated from wa­
ter electrolysis in this case the final Uniprotein® produced originates almost solely from
electricity, water, and CO2. This scenario is selected for its ability to be scaled up easily
in the future, especially if low electricity prices and high operating factors can be achieved.
The final case (S3) will be an attempt at a more optimized industrial symbiosis, where
methane originates from an anaerobic digestion plant coupled with biogas upgrading via
biological methanation (Archaea reactor). Here the produced biogas is mixed with the
CO2 stream from the flue gas carbon capture and enough hydrogen from water electrol­
ysis. The following table 3.1 showcases a summary of the described cases. A process
design, life cycle assessment, and feasibility analysis of each case will follow for some
key performance factors to be calculated.

effective, safe, and sustainable process that produces high­quality products. The simula­
tion environment selected for this endeavor is Aspen Plus v11, a powerful simulator that
offers a comprehensive set of features for simulating a wide range of unit operations and
includes plenty of physical property data for chemicals [62].

tual industrial symbioses some of which are repeated, while others are complex enough
that could constitute entire projects on their own. For time efficiency, simplifications are

The thermodynamic model selected for the main part of every case is Peng Robinson
(PB), an equation of state (EOS), created for predicting the properties of fluids. It is a cubic
equation that relates the pressure, temperature, and volume of a fluid to its molecular
properties. Furthermore, PB is based on the van der Waals equation, but it includes
additional terms that improve its accuracy [63].

3.2.4
Carbon capture
The design of the amine carbon capture facilities is similar for every case. With constraints
discussed in the following section 3.2.5, the only variable of these systems is the mass
and composition of the inlet stream. As a result, the following specifications are the same
for every CC model generated. The components used for every ACC unit are available
in table A.6. Moreover, the thermodynamic model selected is a variation of eNRTL called
eNRTL­RK. eNRTL stands for ”Extended NRTL” and is a more accurate and versatile ver­
sion of NRTL. This thermodynamic model is used to predict the vapor­liquid equilibrium
(VLE) of mixtures and it has been extended to include additional parameters that allow it
to better represent the behavior of real mixtures. The selected method is particularly well­
suited for modeling aqueous electrolyte systems and mixed solvent electrolyte systems
in the presence of water and is commonly used for CO2 capture [68], [69].
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
25
==PAGE:42==
After the selection of the thermodynamic model, the chemistry established in the system
along with the reactions taking place on the absorber and the stripper is available on
table A.7, table A.8 and table A.10. Along with those tables, the design characteristics
of the absorber and the stripper are also available in appendix A.2.1. The final thing that
must be stated is that the heat exchanger used in the model is set to achieve a specific
temperature difference of 10 [oC] between the outlet of the hot stream and the inlet of the
cold one.

3.3
Life Cycle Assessment ­ LCA
Most of the generated data from the process design part are used as inputs for the life
cycle assessment (LCA) of the different cases. LCA is a structured methodology for evalu­
ating the environmental impacts associated with a product or service throughout its entire
life cycle if it is a cradle­to­grave analysis or with just its production if the LCA is cradle­
to­gate. For this part of the thesis, SimaPro is the program selected. It is a software
package that utilizes different inputs for substances and processes at each stage of the
production line for the foreground and the background processes and generates insight­
ful information about the varying impact categories [74]. Some of the key environmental
impact categories that will be researched are:
• Climate change: The release of greenhouse gases into the atmosphere,
• Water pollution: The discharge of pollutants into water bodies, and
• Resource depletion: The use of non­renewable resources.
The reasoning behind the implementation of LCA in industrial projects is to highlight what
needs to change for sustainability to be achieved. Some of the benefits coming with
this assessment are a competitive advantage since companies utilize it to demonstrate
their commitment to sustainability and improved environmental performance through the
identification and reduction of environmental impacts. The following sections will provide
the key information needed for a better understanding of the thought process behind the
model.
3.3.1
Goal Definition
With sustainability becoming a hot topic in the past few years and new legislation and
incentives being implemented, to boost the shift towards non­fossil solutions, the under­
standing of the environmental impact that processes have is imperative. The purpose of
this study is to produce a comparative cradle­to­gate LCA between the different cases of
interest for the production of single­cell protein (SCP) in Denmark. This will have signifi­
cant implications on the viability of the ideas in both an environmental and financial way.
Some other goals of the current LCA are:
• The confirmation, of the original belief before the undertaking of the project, that
the suggested alternations in the sourcing of the feedstocks can result in significant
emission reduction despite being more expensive.
• The estimation of the avoided emissions possible for each of the cases S1 to S3
when compared with S0. This value can and will be utilized as a revenue source
(green quotas) in one of the scenarios researched during the feasibility.
• The creation of a simplified cradle­to­gate model for the assessment of the electro­
chemical ammonia synthesis pathway, that could provide the company with an early
idea of the projected emissions per unit of ammonia produced.
This early level LCA of a conceptual design can only constitute the basis for further work
with its results being used just to provide an overview of the potential sustainability. With
that being said, the audience of this work includes Nitrovolt due to the carbon intensity
calculation for the ammonia product, individuals interested in industrial symbiosis and
sustainability, and potentially Unibio due to the reduced emissions for the production of
the Uniprotein.
28
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:45==
3.3.2
Scope Definition
As was established in chapter 2 the main function of the SCP is the feeding of animals
with great­quality proteins. By­products of the different scenarios include oxygen, carbon
dioxide, nitrogen, heat, etc. Some of those, like the excess heat, can be utilized in the
process, while others will just be sold. The functional unit for every case is the production
of 1000 [kgSCP /h] and as presented in table 3.2 the reference flows include, but are
not limited to, the masses of oxygen, methane, and ammonia. The main reference flow
not included in the table is the electricity consumption which was calculated based on
Aspen and the Danish energy agency [75]. The following fig. 3.7 showcases the system
boundaries for S0 and S1 to S3. It should be pointed out that in methane production all
the options are presented in one box but in reality one or two of those are being used
depending on the case.
Figure 3.6: Schematic illustration of the assumed system boundaries
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
29
==PAGE:46==
As can be seen in the figure, the key difference between the two system boundaries
is the need for transportation of ammonia, and methane in the case S0 and the on­site
production of them at the industrial plants for S1 to S3. The potential environmental benefit
of the elimination of transportation emissions is the main driver behind this idea.
Following the system boundaries, during an LCA its representatives must be stated. Its
three aspects are geographical, time­related, and technology­related.
Given that the
assessment is cradle­to­gate only the sourcing of minerals, chemicals, and catalysts is
location­dependent since there are no available options for those in SimaPro (regarding
Denmark). Despite that, for these instances, European data are implemented mostly from
Germany. The data points utilized are all available in the literature of the past 10 years
rendering them relatively representative of the current situation. Finally, with many units
not modeled in SimaPro before there is a higher level of uncertainty in this regard. More
precisely the sub­projects that correspond to PEM [76], Nitrovolt, Membrane permeation
[77], [78], catalytic [79], and biological methanation [80], [81] had to be produced from
scratch and therefore, technology­wise, the project is as representative as the informa­
tion available in the literature currently.
Before the Life cycle inventory chapter, it should be stated that there are two options for
the type of analysis, midpoint and endpoint. In midpoint analysis, the impact factors are
provided with their units, while in the endpoint they are categorized as either harmful to
humans (unit: [DALY]) or to the environment (unit: [PDF*m2*y]) based on a predetermined
factor. With the main goal of this LCA being the comparison of the different scenarios
regarding their impact in varying categories, the midpoint results are of higher significance.
This LCA study is a comparative analysis evaluating the potential environmental impacts
of a specific functional unit. After modeling the processes in SimaPro, ReCiPe 2016
Midpoint analysis is used to generate the main results available below.
• Climate change short and long term [kgCO2.eq]
• Fossil and nuclear energy use [MJdeprived]
• Mineral resources used [kgdeprived]
• Freshwater ecotoxicity [CTUe]
• Human toxicity cancer and non­cancer [CTUh]
• Terrestrial acidification [kgSO2.eq]
3.3.3
Life Cycle Inventory
For the modeling of the different systems, many assumptions are made, aiming for a fair
and unbiased representation, which are the following:
• Since the Uloop reactor and the further treatment of the product occurs in every
scenario and given the comparative nature of this LCA, this part is approached as
a blank box.
• Despite by­products being created in main cases, the total emissions will not be
allocated. This means that each of the three chemicals going in the Uloop comes
with all the potential emissions, while these secondary products are assumed to be
emission­free when sold.
• The electricity for each scenario is assumed to be originating from renewable en­
ergy. More precisely, on­shore wind turbine parks are located in Denmark. For this
reason, an operating factor of 60% has been selected for these small­scale plants.
30
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:47==
• The captured CO2 from the flue gas is assumed to be avoided emissions and it is
either sold like in case S1 or recycled towards the methane production facilities in
the other two cases. This is assumed to be possible because a version of a simple
carbon capture unit is added in each of the cases.
• Based on 2.11 the Nitrovolt process includes an electrolyzer, a nitrolyzer, and the
the membrane permeation unit. Apart from the polymer electrolyzer not much in­
formation is available in the literature for the other two parts. For this reason, a
simplified model for the membrane permeation is created and the materials needed
for the Nirtolyzer are assumed to be duplicates of the ones used for the polymer
electrolyzer. Of course the electricity consumption of the system is calculated in
collaboration with Nitrovolot.
• The electrolyzer part is a simplified version of the work from Kay Bareiß et. al. [76].
• For case S0 the electricity (assumed renewable), methane, ammonia, and oxygen
consumption are the only inputs of interest. Out of those, only oxygen is produced
on­site with a cryogenic facility while the other options are transported from external
producers.
• The final models for each one of the cases comprise of smaller models generated
for the processes that did not already exist in SimaPro.
Following these assumptions, fig. 3.7 is created to provide a better understanding of which
processes are of interest and will be modeled in an attempt for realistic results to be
achieved. As can be seen in the figure all three options for methane production have
been presented, but only one of them is utilized in each instance.
Figure 3.7: Life cycle process tree
There are some tribulations regarding this endeavor given the nature of the project (in­
dustrial symbiosis LCAs are not that many) but also the specific processes researched
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
31
==PAGE:48==
do not have a wide variety of life cycle assessments performed for them. Despite that, an
attempt at realistic and unprejudiced results is made.
The modeling of the system is based on the unit operations used in the process design
part of this thesis. Regarding topics outside of the process design, transportation in cases
S1 to S3 is only included for materials like catalysts and minerals (when available), and ce­
ment and metals needed for the construction. Furthermore, the handling and distribution
are not included in this project given that it is a cradle­to­gate.
3.4
Feasibility study
It is widely accepted that even though sustainability is an important aspect, the financial
viability of projects is equally significant. For this objective to be achieved, knowledge
acquired from the course 46205 Feasibility Studies of Energy projects available at DTU
is implemented [82]. The goal of this part is to highlight the circumstances needed (if
they exist) for the suggested scenarios to become cost­competitive with S0. For this to
become possible, the main economic metric used is the net present value (NPV), based
on which the break­even price can be calculated (for NPV=0). Of course, no company
would ever sell at this minimum selling price (MSP) since the goal is not just to avoid
losses but actually for profit to be achieved. Despite that, this value provides an objective
and comparable basis between the different cases researched. For the financial model
to be created apart from literature review [27], [83], [75] a series of assumptions is used
and are available in the following chapter.
3.4.1
Assumptions
• The lifetime of the project is set at 25 years
• The operating factor is going to be 60% and the entire electricity demand is to be
covered by wind farms.
• Although the operating factor will be 60%, the possibility of full operation at 88.57
[%] will be also researched. This aims to highlight how much the plant would benefit
from a maximum operation where electricity is provided at reasonable prices from
renewables and energy storage units. Under this circumstance, the limiting factor
will be the maximum possible operation of the Uloop which is 31 days of operation
every 35 days [7].
• The electricity price is always hard to predict not only due to the uncertainty that
comes with renewable energy but also due to its constant fluctuations. Despite
that, for the scenarios that will be discussed later on, the levelized cost of electricity
(LCoE) is based on sources like IRENA and IEA reports, projects funded by the
European Union like Store and Go, and further literature review [10], [48], [83].
• An important contributor to the cost of cases S1 and S2 is expected to be the elec­
tricity price. Usually, in these types of projects wind farms or solar panels are also
installed for renewable electricity generation, but because the modeling of such a
system had to be detailed and in­depth a simple LCoE price from on­shore wind
farms will be the input used.
• In the case of the anaerobic digestion plant, the operating factor is set to be 90.4
[%], and a storage facility for the methane is included in the pricing. As a result,
the biomass feedstock demand is estimated for the hourly demand and annual con­
sumption of Uloop to be met.
32
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:49==
• The pricing of ammonia via the nitrovolt technology is performed by the company
itself, since they have already created an in­depth techno­economic evaluation of
the process. As a result, the capital and operational costs corresponding to this part
are included in their final ammonia price of 1350 [$/t].
• Based on the desired production stated in table 3.2, 5 Uloop reactors are needed
for the current project. The costing of the Unibio process costing and labor pric­
ing are based on previous work by Petersen, Leander A. H. Petersen [6], Rasmus
Abildgaard Fischer [7] and Jonas Thorsager Lassen [8].
• The scaling factor (sf) used in the different pricing calculations is on average equal
to 0.7.
• The sourcing or selling of carbon dioxide is going to be in the form/price of BECCS
or bioenergy carbon capture and storage, meaning that the prices will have a spe­
cific range higher than the one from flue gases and lower than the carbon dioxide
originating for direct air carbon capture (DAC) [84].
• The economic parameters outside of capital and operational costs will remain con­
stant as can be seen in table 3.5. The only value that could be important to be further
investigated is the Discount rate (WACC), which although is commonly around 6 [%],
for new or untried projects tends to be higher.
• The capital costs of processes, used in the analysis, include also the heaters, cool­
ers and other surrounding units needed for the correct operation of the system

3.4.2
Cost estimation
The two main categories of costing are capital investment and operational costs. The
first one corresponds to the initial investment made for the construction, machinery, and
installation, while the second one includes the feedstocks, maintenance of the facilities,
salaries of the personnel, and other additional costs needed for the day­to­day operation.
Capital costs ­ CAPEX
The investment costs for the units needed for each case are available in appendix A.2.3.
The values presented in the tables of the appendix include the lifetime aspect of the units
and also the aforementioned scaling factor which results in lowered cost with increased
capacities [75]. The equations needed for the calculation in this chapter are:
pricei = priceref ∗( sizei

)sf
(3.26)

lti
(3.28)
where i: a unit of the process.
Operational cost ­ OPEX
The operating expenses include the annual costs that are mandatory for the facility to op­
erate. Operating costs can be separated into variable, which are the electricity, minerals,
and feedstocks, and fixed which corresponds to mostly the maintenance of the facilities.
The feedstock flows and electricity needs are generated from the process design part of
the analysis, literature review, and calculations based on the Danish technology catalog
inputs [75]. The demanded minerals by the Uloop process are presented in table A.2 on
the appendix A.1.1, while the personnel needed and the corresponding salaries are based
on the work of Rasmus Abildgaard Fischer [7] and Jonas Thorsager Lassen [8]. Finally,
the maintenance of the units is set equal to 2% of the capital cost for the units that data
are not available.
In contrast to the operational costs, the side revenue streams of the different systems
result in the reduction of the final cost of production for the SCP. These revenue streams
are significantly different for each scenario, but the available options are oxygen, BECCS,
and potentially heat.

Case S3
In S3 the produced biogas from the anaerobic digestion facility is mixed with the carbon
dioxide obtained from the flue gas and enough hydrogen to meet the desired molar ratio
before the mixture is fed to the biological methanation reactor. By implementing a storage
unit the capacity factors between the anaerobic digestion and the remaining facility can
be equalized. With this assumption and given that the design specifications are the same
as case S1, the feedstocks needed are the following.

4.2
Life cycle assesment
Based on the methods stated before and the models generated from the available informa­
tion in the literature table 4.13, fig. 4.6 and fig. A.19 are produced. Before the comparison
of the results of cases S0 to S3, a few remarks for each case can be stated. Regarding
the short­term climate change indicator every scenario had a main contributor with the
cryogenic facility being the main one for S0 and S1 (55% and 60% accordingly), while
the catalytic methanation (65% of total emissions) and the anaerobic digestion facility (al­
most 50%) constitute the main pollutants in S2 and S3. A similar breakdown analysis can
be performed for every one of the presented indicators, but similar trends are observed.
Lastly, it should be mentioned that the main reason behind this LCA is the estimation of
the avoided carbon dioxide emissions that can potentially be achieved by these green
alternatives.
The selected indicators mentioned in the chapter 3 are presented in the following table
and more visually with the radar graph.

A good example that highlights the need for further work on the LCA, comes from the
uncertainty that many of these technologies generate and it is the CI score calculated
for hydrogen produced from water electrolysis. According to the literature the price is
projected to be between 10 and 32 [gCO2eq/MJ] or 1.20 to 3.60 [kgCO2eq/kgH2] [90],[91],
while the created model calculates a CI score of 0.75 [kgCO2eq/kgH2] when electricity
provided only by renewables.

Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:65==
Concluding the life cycle assessment part, based on the electricity consumption provided
by Nitrovolt and the assumptions stated in the methods, the final carbon intensity of their
technology is 0.512 [kgCO2.eq/kgNH3]. The main contributor to these emissions is the on­
shore wind farms used at around 60[%] of the total. This is a very significant reduction,
almost 80 [%], compared to the HB process. Although promising, this result must be
further validated because in case it is correct the attractiveness of the technology will
further increase.
4.3
Feasibility
In the feasibility section of the results, the financial viability of each pathway will be ana­
lyzed. As described in the previous chapter, the break­even prices for specific outcomes
will be calculated. After the sensitivity analysis, a box plot of potential ranges for each
case’s break­even price will be presented. Finally, based on the results of the early­level
life cycle assessment presented, the price of green quotas in [$/tCO2] will be calculated
for the suggested options to become cost­competitive with the unsustainable one. The
capital and operational costs for the different units will be available in the appendix A.2.3.
Based on the Aspen and SimaPro results, table 4.15 is produced to present the significant
inlets, outlets, and avoided streams that can have a financial impact on the process. It
must be stated that the negative values represent the streams that are leaving the system
as revenues, while the oxygen demand, in cases S0 and S1, is covered by a cryogenic
facility that is considered during the capital and operational cost calculations.

Following this base case, a sensitivity analysis of the break­even price (MSP) is conducted
for the key cost contributor of each case to be highlighted. The plot for each varying
factor can be seen in appendix A.2.3, while in the main part, the following tornado graphs
are used because they provide a better visual representation of the financial impacts.
Although the operating factor of the system is fixed at 60 [%], to match or at least be close
to the potential of renewable electricity production, it is expected that the increase of this
parameter will have a very positive effect in every case. For this reason, the operating
factor is dismissed as a potential key contributor. Lastly, whenever the orange or blue
bars are not shown is because this variable does not have an effect on the MSP for the
specific case.
From fig. 4.8, it is clear that most of the parameters of interest for the following case have
very low or zero effects in the conventional approach. This was to be expected given the
fossil origin of most of the feedstocks. Furthermore, it seems that this system is highly
dependent on the capital cost above all else. This means that a better or more efficient
utilization of materials is needed.
50
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
==PAGE:67==
Figure 4.8: Tornado graph for the sensitivity of the MSPSCP case S0
In the case of methane production solely from anaerobic digestion the feedstock and by­
products of the process constitute the most significant cost drivers. This is an expected
trend for the biomass variable given the great amount needed for the production of the
desired amount of methane. Regarding the digestate, it is probably a combination of
a high assumed price and a significant amount of side­stream production. In addition,
the BECCS price can play an important role in reducing the market value of the product,
especially under the assumption that the high demand for carbon dioxide streams in the
future can potentially make it a commodity and drive its cost higher.
Figure 4.9: Tornado graph for the sensitivity of the MSPSCP case S1
When this biological source of methane is swapped to catalytic methanation in a power­to­
x format the remarks are the exact opposite. More specifically, the biomass and digestate
prices have no effect and the diver of the cost is the electricity price due to the high con­
sumption from the water electrolyzer. With that being said and from the graphs produced
during the sensitivity, it seems that without additional incentives, synthetic protein produc­
tion is not going to be cost­competitive soon.

==PAGE:68==
Figure 4.10: Tornado graph for the sensitivity of the MSPSCP case S2
In the final case, the main driver of cost is again the electricity due to the capacity of
the electrolyzer needed for hydrogen production. It is also interesting that the biomass
and digestate contribution is significantly lower when compared to the case S1 which is
expected since not only the anaerobic digestion facility is smaller but also the electricity
consumption and price are a lot higher. A final remark, that is true for every case, is that
the increase in the operating factor is very beneficial for the final price of the SCP mostly
because of the greater production achieved. Of course, this parameter is not expected
to drastically change but the results are very enlightening, regarding the need for further
innovation in the field of sustainable electricity production.
Figure 4.11: Tornado graph for the sensitivity of the MSPSCP case S3

higher end of the box plot. The values used are available in table 4.19, with the operating
factor being 70 [%] mostly to showcase that even in such an ideal scenario the price
difference is still notable.

The most important aspect of the following fig. 4.12 is the length of each box and not really
to higher and lower achieved values. This is because it was expected that the MSPs for
the sustainable options would be higher than the conventional for almost every combina­
tion of values in the range established in the previous table. What really can be extracted
from this graph is the sensitivity and uncertainty these scenarios showcase based on
the selected variables. For instance, the bar that corresponds to case S2 highlights how
volatile this suggestion is towards the electricity and operating factor changes. Therefore,
the remark that can be made here is that the pathway that showcases the greatest po­
tential at the moment is S1. A result that was expected given the lower dependence on
electricity cost along with the very cheap feedstocks used, a combination that results to a
low fluctuation of its final value.

become more appealing. Based on this assumption, new break­even costs are calculated

5
Conclusion
During the thesis, a multidimensional study of potential small­scale industrial symbiosis
plants was carried out. The spark that started this project was the innovative idea for am­
monia production proposed by Nitrovolt which perfectly supplemented the goal of sustain­
able production of chemicals. Three progressively more sustainable cases were analyzed
in an attempt for some key performance indicators to be produced and a comprehensive
comparison between them to be achieved.
The original hypothesis was that the environmental gain that these sustainable alterna­
tives would provide is going to outweigh the economic burden they come with. Moreover,
it was believed that the financing of such cases due to the lowered impact they have
could even render them cost­competitive with the conventional scenario, where fossil­
originating chemicals are used. The following table 5.1 showcases some of the perfor­
mance indicators generated from this work, while the fig. 5.1 gives a more visual repre­
sentation.
Table 5.1: Final indicators per ton of product
Case S0
Case S1
Case S2
Case S3
Units

[%]
Figure 5.1: Concluding radar graph
By observing the results, it is fair to conclude that further innovation is needed for such sus­
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production
57
==PAGE:74==
tainable plants to operate competitively against the existing options. As was expected,
the suggested improvements drove down the CI score of the final product significantly
by increasing the cost of the generated product. Another reasonable result was the re­
duced energy efficiency that the new systems had because of the exothermic nature of
most of the production processes researched. With that being said the significant drop
in efficiency renders a further energy analysis of the system mandatory, for the losses
to be minimized and the overall efficiency to increase. Lastly, the water and electricity
consumption are bound together and increase in cases S2 and S3 due to the water elec­
trolysis needs.
By observing each case separately, the eProtein scenario or S2 is incompatible with small­
scale operations. The great electricity needs and high capital and operational costs of the
catalytic methanation can be alleviated only by scaling up the process. On the other
hand, the anaerobic digestion case (S1) seems to be the most promising alternative as
a near­term solution. The positive results for this case emanate from the relatively low
dependence on electricity and the cheap feedstocks it operates with. Finally, S3 as was
expected falls somewhere between the two pathways but thanks to its very low carbon
intensity score seems to be the most promising case for future implementation under the
assumption that avoided emissions will be incentivized.
As part of the further work that could sharpen the idea and potentially yield different con­
clusions, apart from an in­depth heat integration, the potential implementation of solid
oxide electrolysis with the catalytic methanation should be investigated. This is because
SOEC is believed to supplement well processes that operate at high temperatures due
to the high­quality heat generated. In addition, AEC could probably be a more feasible
alternative for the provision of hydrogen in case S3 resulting in lower capital and opera­
tional costs. Finally, a more in­depth life cycle assessment of the different stages is vital
for more educated decisions to be made.
58
Industrial symbiosis for sustainable protein production

==PAGE:85==
A.1.4
Ammonia Synthesis
Table A.4: Most common catalysts used over the Haber Bosch process advancements
[47], [101], [102], [103]
Catalysts
Remarks
Osmium
1. One of the first catalysts used, but had low yields.
Uranium
1. Followed after Osmium and was a big improvement, but not cost efficient
Iron Based
1. Usually, promoted structurally with Al2O3 and electronically with potassium (alternatives K2O, CaO, SiO2).
2. Has similar efficiency to the previously used and is much cheaper.
3. The most commonly used catalyst on an industrial scale due to cost.
Ruthenium
1. Supported on oxide (MgO & MgAl2O4) becomes a decent ammonia production catalyst.
2. Supported by graphitized carbon renders it one of the best catalysts available resulting in interest in Ru­based ammonia.
3. Mostly used by Kellogg’s production process (similar to the classic HB with some optimization).
4. The key benefit becomes clear when a substantial amount of ammonia has been produced.
5. Main drawback is the economic aspect of this catalyst since it is a lot more expensive that Iron based.
Cobalt + Molybdenum
(CoMo)
1. It is believed to be a promising alternative but currently has similar efficiency to the classical Iron based catalyst while
being much more expensive.
Figure A.2: Linear scaling between nitrogen­binding energy and activation barrier for N2
dissociation [104]
